<div id="review_332824504" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/332824504" />
    <a title="Mohamed " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086726-mohamed"><img alt="Mohamed " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447159598p2/4086726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/332824504?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mohamed " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mohamed " href="/user/show/4086726-mohamed">Mohamed </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer332824504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7007819853919846133">I came to this book not knowing what to expect; I knew that people spoke highly of Nick Hornby, and that his books elicit thought and introspection, and I was coming from a background of mostly fantasy and sci-fi readings, so I was a bit cautious about approaching the book.<br/><br/>Having said that, I am glad to have taken the plunge, because this is quite honestly one of the most amazing books I've had the pleasure to read. Nick Hornby really nails the mood and the characterization here, and I have noth</span>
  <span id="freeText7007819853919846133" style="display:none">I came to this book not knowing what to expect; I knew that people spoke highly of Nick Hornby, and that his books elicit thought and introspection, and I was coming from a background of mostly fantasy and sci-fi readings, so I was a bit cautious about approaching the book.<br /><br />Having said that, I am glad to have taken the plunge, because this is quite honestly one of the most amazing books I've had the pleasure to read. Nick Hornby really nails the mood and the characterization here, and I have nothing but praise for having managed to create four main characters, each of which having their own unique sets of problems and mannerisms, yet really not so different from humankind in general. You read their thoughts, and their conversations with others, and you just find yourself relating to something Martin thought, or JJ said, or Jess's cavalier swearing and youthful confusion. It is all very stellar, really. <br /><br />Of note is the ending, which I shall not spoil here, but it was something that I was worried about all through the book. I feared Hornby would screw it up, or make it unnecessarily depressing or unbelievably happy, but then as I read the ending, I found myself feeling satisfied by the conclusion. It made sense, for those people to come to these conclusions, and I didn't find myself struggling to swallow it, as is sometimes the case. <br /><br />I think that in the near future I might be thinking a bit more about the themes discussed in the book, but this is not the place for it. This is a review, and thus my review of it as that it was a marvelous book, well worth the time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7007819853919846133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Chrissy (The Every Free Chance Reader)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3435480-chrissy-the-every-free-chance-reader"><img alt="Chrissy (The Every Free Chance Reader)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470628515p2/3435480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1670388072?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissy (The Every Free Chance Reader)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissy (The Every Free Chance Reader)" href="/user/show/3435480-chrissy-the-every-free-chance-reader">Chrissy (The Every Free Chance Reader)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1670388072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9057442348864542291">Did I enjoy this book: There’s got to be at least one character in a book that I care about. I was slogging through a modern classic–I won’t name it here–with the least likable set of characters I’d ever encountered. I pushed through to the halfway mark, and then I said, “Why am I doing this? I’m going to find a novel whose people touch my heart.”<br/><br/>I was lucky enough to pick up Nick Hornby’s A Long Way Down. I really enjoyed the book.<br/><br/>The cover of my copy of A Long Way Down shows four pairs of shoe</span>
  <span id="freeText9057442348864542291" style="display:none">Did I enjoy this book: There’s got to be at least one character in a book that I care about. I was slogging through a modern classic–I won’t name it here–with the least likable set of characters I’d ever encountered. I pushed through to the halfway mark, and then I said, “Why am I doing this? I’m going to find a novel whose people touch my heart.”<br /><br />I was lucky enough to pick up Nick Hornby’s A Long Way Down. I really enjoyed the book.<br /><br />The cover of my copy of A Long Way Down shows four pairs of shoes hovering over a blue sky. The wing tips are fallen talk show host Martin’s. The comfortable oxfords are 51-year-old Maureen’s. Teenaged smart-mouth Jess owns the black Keds (and the red socks), and the boots belong to rock star wannabe JJ. These four unlikely compatriots meet atop Topper’s Tower in London one New Year’s Eve. Each climbs to the roof alone, intending to jump and end a life that no longer has meaning.<br /><br />They come down in a pack, on a mission to find Jess’s erstwhile boyfriend. Then, mission accomplished, they find they can neither break their connection nor complete their original mission.<br /><br />The book is told in all four voices, so we get inside each of their heads–miserable screw-up Martin, who had it all and lost it via his own stupidity; self-effacing Maureen, whose life has been spent caring for a son so disabled he has no awareness; the jilted Jess, whose sister is missing–a probable suicide; and musician JJ, who lost his band, his love, and his will to live.<br /><br />This band of misfits screws up regularly and sometimes spectacularly. They’re drifty and lunatic, self-centered and clueless, and yet–I care about each one.<br /><br />Would I recommend it: I recommend A Long Way Down if you want a funny book with likable characters that admits to hope. Don’t look for saccharine, though–true love doesn’t come waltzing through the door, nor do the foolish become wise with the flash of a Hogwarts’ wand. But people on the wrong course do manage, with determination and an odd kind of courage, to turn their baggage-heavy ships. They get by,–yes, they do,–with a little help from their odd cast of friends.<br /><br />As reviewed by Pam at Every Free Chance Books.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://everyfreechance.com/2015/03/guest-review-a-long-way-down-by-nick-hornby.html">http://everyfreechance.com/2015/03/gu...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9057442348864542291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Evelyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19089056-evelyn"><img alt="Evelyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437415588p2/19089056.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1718237064?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evelyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evelyn" href="/user/show/19089056-evelyn">Evelyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781573223027" href="/book/show/460727.A_Long_Way_Down">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1718237064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15323101981290325782">Four strangers meet at arguably the lowest point in their lives and form an unlikely bond. Unlikely because these four people are so very different from each other that under normal circumstances they would never ever cross paths.<br/>I laughed right out loud at several times while reading - not what I expected at all.<br/>I enjoyed how each character took turns telling the story - of their lives, their loves, their sorrows. As the book progressed it was like peeling the layers of an onion, the reader gai</span>
  <span id="freeText15323101981290325782" style="display:none">Four strangers meet at arguably the lowest point in their lives and form an unlikely bond. Unlikely because these four people are so very different from each other that under normal circumstances they would never ever cross paths.<br />I laughed right out loud at several times while reading - not what I expected at all.<br />I enjoyed how each character took turns telling the story - of their lives, their loves, their sorrows. As the book progressed it was like peeling the layers of an onion, the reader gains deeper and deeper insight into each character as we go along.<br />Each character has both a distinct voice and personality as well, and it was written in such a way that they were actually speaking to the reader - loved that.<br />Jesse to the reader after explaining why she was on the roof - if you're old and happy, I can imagine that you'll maybe smile to yourself when you here me going, He broke my heart. You'll remember someone who broke your heart, and you'll think to yourself, Oh, yes, I can remember how that feels. But you can't you smug old git. - then follows the rather lengthy explanation of what Jesse thinks you remember contrasted with what she is experiencing.<br />Maureen to the reader on her reasons for and against - because that's why I was up there-there wasn't quite enough to stop me.<br />Jesse explaining why the bookclub didn't work out - you should try and read the stuff by people who've killed themselves! We started with Virginia Woolf, and I only read like two pages of this book about a lighthouse, but I read enough to know why she killed herself. She killed herself because she couldn't make herself understood......because in the olden days anyone could get a book published because there wasn't so much competition.........whereas now they'd go, No, dear, go away, no one will understand you. Try Pilates or salsa dancing instead.<br />Martin to Jesse in response to her comment that she had not attempted suicide again as she had been busy - "Terrible, isn't it, how that happens? You'll have to block out some time in your diary. Otherwise life will keep getting in the way."<br />Lighthearted, yet thoughtful. This is a keeper for me.<br /><br />2nd read<br />Enjoyed this just as much as the first time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15323101981290325782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1902930507" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1902930507" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2161536-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585182412p2/2161536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1902930507?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/2161536-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1902930507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4000039422040201960">Nick Hornby has such a distinctive voice. For me, the plot of his novels always takes a backseat to simply enjoying his fingerprints all over the pages. It sounds extreme, but I find myself processing his books sentence by sentence rather than chapter by chapter. This particular novel is definitely edgy territory (suicide) but, as usual, Hornby finds a way to somehow skirt the fine line between humor and the devastating realities of loneliness, unfulfilled dreams, lack of internal peace, etc. th</span>
  <span id="freeText4000039422040201960" style="display:none">Nick Hornby has such a distinctive voice. For me, the plot of his novels always takes a backseat to simply enjoying his fingerprints all over the pages. It sounds extreme, but I find myself processing his books sentence by sentence rather than chapter by chapter. This particular novel is definitely edgy territory (suicide) but, as usual, Hornby finds a way to somehow skirt the fine line between humor and the devastating realities of loneliness, unfulfilled dreams, lack of internal peace, etc. that most of us face. Since I reserve my "5" ratings for those books that will stay with me forever, I am giving A Long Way Down a 4. But I thoroughly enjoyed it and recommend it for anybody who wants to take a provocative ride through largely unchartered territory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4000039422040201960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_51776050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer12173461323852123990"><u>a note on misters folds and hornby and why they should be appreciated if not enjoyed. </u><br/><br/>So I have been listening to the new CD by hornby and folds pretty straight for the last day short of the time I have been working or sleeping (I'll get to this specific book be patient). Seldomly do I have any idea that something is coming out in advance. I almost never know if a band I like has a new cd, sometimes until years later, and I learn about a new book by walking by it in the store or karen putting it</span>
  <span id="freeText12173461323852123990" style="display:none"><u>a note on misters folds and hornby and why they should be appreciated if not enjoyed. </u><br /><br />So I have been listening to the new CD by hornby and folds pretty straight for the last day short of the time I have been working or sleeping (I'll get to this specific book be patient). Seldomly do I have any idea that something is coming out in advance. I almost never know if a band I like has a new cd, sometimes until years later, and I learn about a new book by walking by it in the store or karen putting it aside for me. This isn't to say I don't get excited I look forward to the next kundera, pernice, currie like no one's business I just don't know in advance the day they will arrive or if anything is even in the works. Hornby is different, I tend to keep an eye on him. I have been waiting for this CD since an education came out last october. I knew that it had been pushed back by the movie and I prayed that it hadn't simply been canceled. And here's the thing I love Mr. hornby and mr. folds...<br /><br /><u>An interlude relating to this novel. </u> <br />(Spoilers possible, but none that ruin the book as far as I'm concerned)<br /><br />A long way down is probably my favorite novel by hornby, according to an interview I read it is also Mr. fold's favorite novel. The novel is about that period that everyone* has gone through where you really just need to throw yourself off the top of a building but you can't bring yourself to do it. But what happens if everyone else chooses the same night you do to throw themselves off a building shenanigans that's what. <br /><br />What this book lacks is a happy ending. Hornby has not figured it out. He doesn't have the secret of life. The best the book does is offer a weird fact that if suicidal people wait 90 days then the feeling passes and they don't kill themselves. <br /><br /><u>Why mr. hornby is important</u><br /><br />You know how there are a bunch of hot young male chefs in england who got men to start learnign to cook and caring about what they eat. Hornby is along the same reasoning. His books venture into an avenue that a lot of male british life tends to avoid. Given my understanding of this comes heavily through novels and research on body image in men. But there is a feeling in the general pub culture that men don't really need to express emotions or have feelings about their own bodies etc because they can just bash each other in the head with their foreheads(if someone can explain to me why this happens it would be appreciated). We see this as well in America, I mean we(I) meet so many men who say "I don't want a relationship I just want to fuck a bunch of women" who clearly (sorry guys) don't actually feel that way. Eventually they realize this usually after several extremely unpleasant one night stands. I get where this comes from. I get that in america and england having feelings just isn't manly. (Once you get to intellectuals it is a bit more accepted though). Hornby allows an outlit for men to experience these feelings. I think it is a special kind of catharsis to be projected into a world where you can feel things that you aren't allowed to feel anywhere else. <br /><br />Yes, I admit in america the readership of hornby skews female and i think that is because it reads as a less direct annoying form of chick lit. The books are about feelings and relationships. But they are generally about ones that aren't working or pleasant. I mean if we think of an education well that just turned out to be a god damn disaster. This for me comes down to a level of honesty that I think you really don't get other places:<br /><br /><i>"It's no good pretending that any relationship has a future if your record collections disagree violently or if your favorite films wouldn't even speak to each other if they met at a party."<br /><br />"A man who wants to die feels angry and full of life and desperate and bored and exhausted, all at the same time; he wants to fight everyone, and he wants to curl up in a ball and hide in a cupboard somewhere. He wants to say sorry to everyone, and he wants everyone to know just how badly they've all let him down."<br /><br />"...I've committed to nothing...and that's just suicide...by tiny, tiny increments." <br /><br />"Telling me I can do anything I want is like pulling the plug out of the bath and then telling the water it can go anywhere it wants. Try it, and see what happens."</i><br /><br />I think it's fine to disagree with the things hornby says or the candy coated ways that he says them, but I think to dismiss him is a mistake. Hornby is talking to a population. Probably younger people, it is in fact a fairly "quarter life crisis message" but a good one and an important one. I think this should be appreciated, and if nothing else we can celebrate the fact that fame hasn't caused him to start pooping out books like palahniuk he still writes at about the same quality level he always has. <br /><br />(A side note he also admits to having terrible taste in literature, but this is another conversation which would require me to have his books of essays in front of me)<br /><br /><u>Back to my original point</u><br /><br />Now here I could go through and talk about all the amazing ben folds songs and say all the same things I said about hornby about him, but instead how about I say a few things and you assume my argument for him is basically the same. <br /><br /><i>Everybody knows<br />It hurts to grow up<br />And everybody does<br /><br /><br />I'm not saying the effort is a waste of time - but I<br />Just love you for the things you couldn't change Though you've tried</i><br /><br /><u>A note on lonely avenue</u><br /><br />The new cd by hornby and folds brings them together and since the point of view and audience both tend to be at least from my perception twenty somethings you end up with something that at least to me seems quite lovely(lyrics all by hornby as far as I can tell):<br /><br /><i>Some guy on the net thinks I suck and he should know<br />He's got his own blog<br />I'm a loser<br />I'm a poser<br />Yeah really<br />It's over<br />I mean it and I quit<br />Everything I write is shit<br /><br />So we talk and it turns out we don't believe in abortion<br />And sex outside marriage is against our religion<br />And when I try to tell them I'm 18 years old,<br />They say "Levi it's too late, you've got to do as you're told"<br /><br />It's so easy from above<br />You can really see it all<br />People who belong together<br />Lost and sad and small<br />But there's nothing to be done for them<br />It doesn't work that way<br />Sure we all have soulmates but we walk past them every day, oh no</i><br /><br />and what is it that I think is great about this, because clearly with all this crap I have typed it must be something. I think it really captures a level of confusion that typifies my generation at this particular moment. And perhaps more widely all people at all times. I mean I have never been as old as hornby so maybe it never goes away. And there is the moment that I think I personally truly appreciate in the cd and in this book. the confusion, the depression, the fear never go away. You either learn to live with them (this book) or you change the situation (slam, many times) and learn to live with a different kind of confusion and emotional well issues. <br /><br />The fact is, yeah folds and hornby tend toward a happy tone but it isn't all coming up roses, and it isn't so weird it is unbelievable. It has a strong relationship to reality, which is perhaps why some people find it so boring. <br /><br />*this is just an outright lie. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12173461323852123990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11932531339960361842">I’ve been a reader for so long but never have I ever read a book quite like this. <br/><br/>I seen this book in a charity shop and read the blurb and it called to me, those few lines gripped me and had me needing to read this book and honestly, I’m so glad I snatched it up and decided to read it. <br/><br/>This book follows the lives of 4 people who ultimately, wanted to end their lives on New Year’s Eve but after the first up on the tower block was interrupted and the other two joined everything went in a differen</span>
  <span id="freeText11932531339960361842" style="display:none">I’ve been a reader for so long but never have I ever read a book quite like this. <br /><br />I seen this book in a charity shop and read the blurb and it called to me, those few lines gripped me and had me needing to read this book and honestly, I’m so glad I snatched it up and decided to read it. <br /><br />This book follows the lives of 4 people who ultimately, wanted to end their lives on New Year’s Eve but after the first up on the tower block was interrupted and the other two joined everything went in a different direction.<br /><br />Martin, Maureen, Jess and JJ each have their own problems, their own reasons for wanting to end their lives and though out this book we get to see why and their stories are sad to read, we get to know them more and love them for who they are. <br /><br />This book was so much more than I thought it would be, I couldn’t put it down at all and ended up devouring it within hours. It was funny, addictive and sad all rolled up into one amazing read. <br /><br />Nick Hornby truly is an amazing writer, surprisingly this is my first read from this author but most definitely won’t be my last. I can’t wait to see what his other books are like. <br /></span>

<div id="review_1733864037" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1733864037" />
    <a title="Ramsey Hootman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5516415-ramsey-hootman"><img alt="Ramsey Hootman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442648237p2/5516415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1733864037?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ramsey Hootman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ramsey Hootman" href="/user/show/5516415-ramsey-hootman">Ramsey Hootman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1733864037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11090464670676886367">You know how there are books that you know you'd love if you didn't know so much about a certain topic? Because the author clearly didn't bother do his or her research, and because you happen to know a lot about the topic, all of the glaring holes basically ruin the entire narrative for you? This is one of those. I hate that it was, because I loved About a Boy and I think Nick Hornby is fantastically funny. And yet. <br/><br/>The premise here is that four people encounter each other on the top of a buildi</span>
  <span id="freeText11090464670676886367" style="display:none">You know how there are books that you know you'd love if you didn't know so much about a certain topic? Because the author clearly didn't bother do his or her research, and because you happen to know a lot about the topic, all of the glaring holes basically ruin the entire narrative for you? This is one of those. I hate that it was, because I loved About a Boy and I think Nick Hornby is fantastically funny. And yet. <br /><br />The premise here is that four people encounter each other on the top of a building on New Year's Eve, all planning to commit suicide. They all come to know one another and (sort of) help each other in a variety of unexpected ways. All of the characters agree that the individual with the most legitimate reason for offing herself is the fifty-something mother of a profoundly disabled son. And that's where it falls apart.<br /><br />I was not at all surprised by the way the characters refer to Matty, the now-adult son who is described as nothing more than a "vegetable." Hornby's trademark is profoundly flawed characters, which is something that I love about his writing. However, I assumed that as the book went on, we would get flashes of humanity here and there for Matty, as we see with all the other characters. That was absolutely not the case. As the narrative went on and he remained nothing more than an object, a prop, I became more and more uncomfortable, and began to feel that this was not simply the point of view of the characters, but of the author as well. It began to feel as if the author did not even see Matty as human. <br /><br />What really got me, though, was just the profound lack of accuracy about people with these kinds of disabilities. Let's just review the setup. Maureen, the mother, has been doing nothing but caring for her son Matty since he was born. She is incredibly isolated because she cares for him alone. He does not appear to have any medical problems, he just "exists." She's not wealthy, but occasionally she pays to leave him at a care place. So, her problem, as presented in the book, is her profound isolation. At the end it's solved by her getting to know a couple of nurses from the care facility, leaving Matty more often, and getting a part-time job.<br /><br />But honestly, this setup just isn't even possible. It doesn't even work in the book. Even though she supposedly cares for Matty round the clock, she goes to church on a weekly basis. And her friends at church mostly do not know about Matty. So... how does that work? Where is Matty then? Matty is also described as being tall and definitely adult (19, I think), so how on earth could Maureen possibly care for him by herself? How would this small woman manage to transfer him from his wheelchair to his bed? (I should note that in the narrative, he is always just 'in his chair,' as if he resides here permanently.) He is described as being basically unresponsive in every way, no movement whatsoever. There's no detail about whether his eyes open, if he can swallow on his own, or what. But either way, it doesn't work out. If he's essentially comatose, there is no way Maureen would be able to take care of him by herself, and no way he wouldn't have frequent medical issues that would require professional care and hospitalization. Is he eating through a tube? Does he have a stoma? If he can't shift his own weight, what about bedsores? Bone density loss? Accidental broken bones? There's just no way that Maureen would be stuck at home as his only carer, alone. Or maybe he's not THAT disabled. Maybe he can eat, maybe he can shift, maybe he can move a bit? In which case, the author has done Matty a profound disservice by erasing his character. Even a non-verbal person is capable of having a personality, but Matty is very specifically denied that. He is not a person, he is a prop.<br /><br />In the end, Maureen "learns" that leaving Matty in the care of strangers is okay-- and here's the kicker-- not because she needs to take care of herself first, but because she realizes there's literally no difference between her level of care and that at an institution. Anyone who knows a family with a severely disabled child can tell you this isn't even remotely true. And it's not just a delusion of family members who want to believe that they can make a difference. It's demonstrably better, both emotionally and medically, for a child to be cared for by someone who loves them, knows their body and medical conditions inside out, and can best judge what they need. So much so that where I live (California) parents are paid to be caregivers so that their children can stay out of institutionalized care.<br /><br />Basically, the Maureen/Matty narrative reads like someone's very ignorant assumption of what life must be like for a profoundly disabled person and his carer. It is a "worst-case" scenario that literally does not and could not exist. This is ignorant, and it perpetuates harmful stereotypes about people with disabilities.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11090464670676886367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1996201164" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1996201164" />
    <a title="Ange H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21166492-ange-h"><img alt="Ange H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509888949p2/21166492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1996201164?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ange H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ange H" href="/user/show/21166492-ange-h">Ange H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1996201164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1086718462240698537">Four people decide to commit suicide by jumping off a roof at midnight on New Year's Eve. They don't know each other, it's just that they've all chosen the same roof and the same time to jump. (This patent lack of originality only makes them feel worse about themselves.) Since the moment has clearly passed, after some awkward discussion they eventually agree to go back down and have a drink. <br/><br/>With this absurd beginning, we meet Martin, Maureen, JJ, and Jess. They each confess the reasons that bro</span>
  <span id="freeText1086718462240698537" style="display:none">Four people decide to commit suicide by jumping off a roof at midnight on New Year's Eve. They don't know each other, it's just that they've all chosen the same roof and the same time to jump. (This patent lack of originality only makes them feel worse about themselves.) Since the moment has clearly passed, after some awkward discussion they eventually agree to go back down and have a drink. <br /><br />With this absurd beginning, we meet Martin, Maureen, JJ, and Jess. They each confess the reasons that brought them to this point. Martin was a TV talk show host who lost everything - and went to jail - after sleeping with an (unbeknownst to him) underage girl. Maureen, a single mother with a severely disabled grown son, is overwhelmed by loneliness and the endless stress of caregiving. JJ is broke, has lost his girlfriend, his best friend, and his dream of making it as a musician. Jess is privileged but desperately unhappy and generally screwed up. <br /><br />These four people couldn't have less in common, except the one big thing that brought them together. They decide to form a support group of sorts, agreeing to meet regularly and then after 90 days, reevaluate the whole jump off the roof idea.<br /><br />I laughed out loud a LOT while reading this book. The pacing was uneven and the end just a tiny bit anticlimactic, but I recommend it highly if you enjoy dark humor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1086718462240698537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/65913-tim">Tim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65913-tim?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2201999138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1912558535687217447">There's a terrifying passage in David Foster Wallace's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest' title='Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace' rel='nofollow noopener'>Infinite Jest</a> that has stuck with me, about suicidal depression and the importance of someone staying up with you and being there through the long night until the dawn. This book, about four people who meet attempting to jump to their deaths from the top of a London tower, is ... not that. In general, Hornby doesn't really attempt to capture the internal feeling of severe depression, focusing more on the interactions between the four. It end</span>
  <span id="freeText1912558535687217447" style="display:none">There's a terrifying passage in David Foster Wallace's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest" title="Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace" rel="nofollow noopener">Infinite Jest</a> that has stuck with me, about suicidal depression and the importance of someone staying up with you and being there through the long night until the dawn. This book, about four people who meet attempting to jump to their deaths from the top of a London tower, is ... not that. In general, Hornby doesn't really attempt to capture the internal feeling of severe depression, focusing more on the interactions between the four. It ends up playing quite a bit like a deliberately quirky set-up for an indie film (which... <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0458413/" rel="nofollow noopener">well</a>). The character dynamics and dialogue are mostly well done, but it's hard not to feel like something important is missing here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1912558535687217447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DJHNVQnCwBnjqEkWQu9rJLGdh9Nm0+9sq6NjqsBFEHbK2MYd+hPL1btRrjQFZPFK+rLc+rUmNqobRjLFaMNe7A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bvKcxmtsHgnuPvWTQYZZ4Vt5u4+yLNdwCCSonL4Q/7Oou5eOmL0VxbbHErEGDcOPEFbgpmHZDra4wfnzFpaxKQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18322599059227169222">Nick Hornby is the author of the novels <i>A Long Way Down, Slam, <i>How to Be Good</i>, High Fidelity</i>, and <i>About a Boy</i>, and the memoir <i>Fever Pitch</i>. He is also the author of <i>Songbook</i>, a finalist for a National Book Critics Circle Award, <i>Shakespeare Wrote for Money</i>, and <i>The Polysyllabic Spree</i>, as well as the editor of the short-story collection <i>Speaking with the Angel</i>. He is a recipient of the American Acade</span>
  <span id="freeText18322599059227169222" style="display:none">Nick Hornby is the author of the novels <i>A Long Way Down, Slam, <i>How to Be Good</i>, High Fidelity</i>, and <i>About a Boy</i>, and the memoir <i>Fever Pitch</i>. He is also the author of <i>Songbook</i>, a finalist for a National Book Critics Circle Award, <i>Shakespeare Wrote for Money</i>, and <i>The Polysyllabic Spree</i>, as well as the editor of the short-story collection <i>Speaking with the Angel</i>. He is a recipient of the American Academy of Arts and Letters’ E. M. Forster Award and the winner of the 2003 Orange Word International Writers’ London Award. Among his many other honors and awards, four of his titles have been named <i>New York Times</i> Notable Books. A film written by Hornby, <i>An Education</i> – shown at the Sundance Film Festival to great acclaim – was the lead movie at the 2009 Toronto Film Festival and distributed by Sony that fall. That same September, the author published his latest novel, <i>Juliet, Naked</i> to wide acclaim. Hornby lives in North London. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18322599059227169222" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/2961964-a-long-way-down">Trivia About A Long Way Down</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2961964-a-long-way-down">Quotes from A Long Way Down</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I don't know you. The only thing I know about you is, you're reading this. I don't know if your happy or not; I don't know whether you're young or not. I sort of hope you're young and sad. If you're old and happy, I can imagine that you'll smile to yourself when you hear me going, he broke my heart. You'll remember someone who broke your heart, and you'll think to yourself, Oh yes, i remember how that feels. But you can't, you smug old git. Oh you'll remember feeling sort of plesantly sad. You might remember listening to music and eating chocolates in your room, or walking along the embankment on your own, wrapped up in a winter coat and feeling lonely and brave. But can you remember how with every mouthful of food it felt like you were biting into your own stomach? Can you remember the taste of red wine as it came back up and into the toilet bowl? Can you remember dreaming every night that you were still together, that he was talking to you gently and touching you, so that every morning when you woke up you had to go through it all over again?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/225662-i-don-t-know-you-the-only-thing-i-know-about">839 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;How do people, like, not curse? How is it possible? There are these gaps in speech where you just have to put a "fuck." I'll tell you who the most admirable people in the world are: newscasters. If that was me, I'd be like, "And the motherfuckers flew the fucking plane right into the Twin Towers." How could you not, if you're a human being? Maybe they're not so admirable. Maybe they're robot zombies.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/4352-how-do-people-like-not-curse-how-is-it-possible">679 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/2961964-a-long-way-down">More quotes&hellip;</a>
